<h1 class="firstHeading mw-first-heading" id="firstHeading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">Brain</span></h1>
<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="contentSub2"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Organ that controls the nervous system in vertebrates and most invertebrates</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">This article is about the brains of all types of animals, including humans. For information specific to the human brain, see <a href="/wiki/Human_brain" title="Human brain">Human brain</a>. For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Brain_(disambiguation)" title="Brain (disambiguation)">Brain (disambiguation)</a> and <a class="mw-redirect mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Brains_(disambiguation)" title="Brains (disambiguation)">Brains (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1066479718">.mw-parser-output .mw-parser-output .infobox-full-data,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-below{text-align:center}</style><table class="infobox"><tbody><tr><th class="infobox-above" colspan="2" Wikidata</a>]</small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>A <b>brain</b> is an <a href="/wiki/Organ_(biology)" title="Organ (biology)">organ</a> that serves as the center of the <a href="/wiki/Nervous_system" title="Nervous system">nervous system</a> in all <a href="/wiki/Vertebrate" title="Vertebrate">vertebrate</a> and most <a href="/wiki/Invertebrate" title="Invertebrate">invertebrate</a> animals. It is located in the <a href="/wiki/Head" title="Head">head</a>, usually close to the sensory organs for <a href="/wiki/Sense" title="Sense">senses</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Visual_perception" title="Visual perception">vision</a>. It is the most complex organ in a vertebrate's body. In a human, the <a href="/wiki/Cerebral_cortex" title="Cerebral cortex">cerebral cortex</a> contains approximately 14–16 billion <a href="/wiki/Neuron" title="Neuron">neurons</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Saladin11_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-Saladin11-1">[1]</a></sup> and the estimated number of neurons in the <a href="/wiki/Cerebellum" title="Cerebellum">cerebellum</a> is 55–70 billion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JCN_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-JCN-2">[2]</a></sup> Each neuron is connected by <a href="/wiki/Synapse" title="Synapse">synapses</a> to several thousand other neurons. These neurons typically communicate with one another by means of long fibers called <a href="/wiki/Axon" title="Axon">axons</a>, which carry trains of signal pulses called <a href="/wiki/Action_potential" title="Action potential">action potentials</a> to distant parts of the brain or body targeting specific recipient cells.
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Physiologically" title="Physiologically">Physiologically</a>, brains exert centralized control over a body's other organs. They act on the rest of the body both by generating patterns of muscle activity and by driving the secretion of chemicals called <a href="/wiki/Hormone" title="Hormone">hormones</a>. This centralized control allows rapid and coordinated responses to changes in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Environment_(biophysical)" title="Environment (biophysical)">environment</a>. Some basic types of responsiveness such as <a href="/wiki/Reflex" title="Reflex">reflexes</a> can be mediated by the spinal cord or peripheral <a href="/wiki/Ganglion" title="Ganglion">ganglia</a>, but sophisticated purposeful control of behavior based on complex sensory input requires the information integrating capabilities of a centralized brain.
</p><p>The operations of individual brain cells are now understood in considerable detail but the way they cooperate in ensembles of millions is yet to be solved.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> Recent models in modern neuroscience treat the brain as a biological computer, very different in mechanism from an electronic computer, but similar in the sense that it acquires information from the surrounding world, stores it, and processes it in a variety of ways.
</p><p>This article compares the properties of brains across the entire range of animal species, with the greatest attention to vertebrates. It deals with the <a href="/wiki/Human_brain" title="Human brain">human brain</a> insofar as it shares the properties of other brains. The ways in which the human brain differs from other brains are covered in the human brain article. Several topics that might be covered here are instead covered there because much more can be said about them in a human context. The most important that are covered in the human brain article are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brain_disease" title="Brain disease">brain disease</a> and the effects of <a href="/wiki/Brain_damage" title="Brain damage">brain damage</a>.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Anatomy">Anatomy</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:264px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Section_through_olfactory_bulb_16_days_old_rat_brain.jpg"><img alt="a blob with a blue patch in the center, surrounded by a white area, surrounded by a thin strip of dark-colored material" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="356" data-file-width="600" decoding="async" height="155" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Section_through_olfactory_bulb_16_days_old_rat_brain.jpg/262px-Section_through_olfactory_bulb_16_days_old_rat_brain.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Section_through_olfactory_bulb_16_days_old_rat_brain.jpg/393px-Section_through_olfactory_bulb_16_days_old_rat_brain.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Section_through_olfactory_bulb_16_days_old_rat_brain.jpg/524px-Section_through_olfactory_bulb_16_days_old_rat_brain.jpg 2x" width="262"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Section_through_olfactory_bulb_16_days_old_rat_brain.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Cross section of the <a href="/wiki/Olfactory_bulb" title="Olfactory bulb">olfactory bulb</a> of a rat, stained in two different ways at the same time: one stain shows neuron cell bodies, the other shows receptors for the <a href="/wiki/Neurotransmitter" title="Neurotransmitter">neurotransmitter</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/GABA" title="GABA">GABA</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>The shape and size of the brain varies greatly between species, and identifying common features is often difficult.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shepherd_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-Shepherd-4">[4]</a></sup> Nevertheless, there are a number of principles of brain architecture that apply across a wide range of species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sporns2010_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sporns2010-5">[5]</a></sup> Some aspects of brain structure are common to almost the entire range of animal species;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Başar2010_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-Başar2010-6">[6]</a></sup> others distinguish "advanced" brains from more primitive ones, or distinguish vertebrates from invertebrates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shepherd_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-Shepherd-4">[4]</a></sup>
</p><p>The simplest way to gain information about brain anatomy is by visual inspection, but many more sophisticated techniques have been developed. Brain tissue in its natural state is too soft to work with, but it can be hardened by immersion in alcohol or other <a href="/wiki/Fixation_(histology)" title="Fixation (histology)">fixatives</a>, and then sliced apart for examination of the interior. Visually, the interior of the brain consists of areas of so-called <a href="/wiki/Grey_matter" title="Grey matter">grey matter</a>, with a dark color, separated by areas of <a href="/wiki/White_matter" title="White matter">white matter</a>, with a lighter color. Further information can be gained by staining slices of brain tissue with a variety of chemicals that bring out areas where specific types of molecules are present in high concentrations. It is also possible to <a href="/wiki/Histology" title="Histology">examine the microstructure</a> of brain tissue using a microscope, and to trace the pattern of connections from one brain area to another.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Singh2006_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-Singh2006-7">[7]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cellular_structure">Cellular structure</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:263px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Chemical_synapse_schema_cropped.jpg"><img alt='drawing showing a neuron with a fiber emanating from it labeled "axon" and making contact with another cell. An inset shows an enlargement of the contact zone.' class="thumbimage" data-file-height="678" data-file-width="544" decoding="async" height="326" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Chemical_synapse_schema_cropped.jpg/261px-Chemical_synapse_schema_cropped.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Chemical_synapse_schema_cropped.jpg/392px-Chemical_synapse_schema_cropped.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Chemical_synapse_schema_cropped.jpg/523px-Chemical_synapse_schema_cropped.jpg 2x" width="261"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Chemical_synapse_schema_cropped.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Neurons generate electrical signals that travel along their axons. When a pulse of electricity reaches a junction called a <a href="/wiki/Synapse" title="Synapse">synapse</a>, it causes a neurotransmitter chemical to be released, which binds to receptors on other cells and thereby alters their electrical activity.</div></div></div>
<p>The brains of all species are composed primarily of two broad classes of cells: <a href="/wiki/Neuron" title="Neuron">neurons</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neuroglia" title="Neuroglia">glial cells</a>. Glial cells (also known as <i>glia</i> or <i>neuroglia</i>) come in several types, and perform a number of critical functions, including structural support, metabolic support, insulation, and guidance of development. Neurons, however, are usually considered the most important cells in the brain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kandel_2000_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kandel_2000-8">[8]</a></sup>
The property that makes neurons unique is their ability to send signals to specific target cells over long distances.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kandel_2000_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kandel_2000-8">[8]</a></sup> They send these signals by means of an axon, which is a thin protoplasmic fiber that extends from the cell body and projects, usually with numerous branches, to other areas, sometimes nearby, sometimes in distant parts of the brain or body. The length of an axon can be extraordinary: for example, if a <a href="/wiki/Pyramidal_cell" title="Pyramidal cell">pyramidal cell</a> (an excitatory neuron) of the cerebral cortex were magnified so that its cell body became the size of a human body, its axon, equally magnified, would become a cable a few centimeters in diameter, extending more than a kilometer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> These axons transmit signals in the form of electrochemical pulses called action potentials, which last less than a thousandth of a second and travel along the axon at speeds of 1–100 meters per second. Some neurons emit action potentials constantly, at rates of 10–100 per second, usually in irregular patterns; other neurons are quiet most of the time, but occasionally emit a burst of action potentials.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup>
</p><p>Axons transmit signals to other neurons by means of specialized junctions called <a href="/wiki/Synapse" title="Synapse">synapses</a>. A single axon may make as many as several thousand synaptic connections with other cells.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kandel_2000_8-2"><a href="#cite_note-Kandel_2000-8">[8]</a></sup> When an action potential, traveling along an axon, arrives at a synapse, it causes a chemical called a <a href="/wiki/Neurotransmitter" title="Neurotransmitter">neurotransmitter</a> to be released. The neurotransmitter binds to <a href="/wiki/Receptor_(biochemistry)" title="Receptor (biochemistry)">receptor</a> molecules in the membrane of the target cell.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kandel_2000_8-3"><a href="#cite_note-Kandel_2000-8">[8]</a></sup>
</p><p>Synapses are the key functional elements of the brain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ShepherdSOB_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-ShepherdSOB-11">[11]</a></sup> The essential function of the brain is <a href="/wiki/Cell%E2%80%93cell_interaction" title="Cell–cell interaction">cell-to-cell communication</a>, and synapses are the points at which communication occurs. The human brain has been estimated to contain approximately 100 trillion synapses;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> even the brain of a fruit fly contains several million.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> The functions of these synapses are very diverse: some are excitatory (exciting the target cell); others are inhibitory; others work by activating <a href="/wiki/Second_messenger_system" title="Second messenger system">second messenger systems</a> that change the internal <a href="/wiki/Chemistry" title="Chemistry">chemistry</a> of their target cells in complex ways.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ShepherdSOB_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-ShepherdSOB-11">[11]</a></sup> A large number of synapses are dynamically modifiable; that is, they are capable of changing strength in a way that is controlled by the patterns of signals that pass through them. It is widely believed that <a href="/wiki/Synaptic_plasticity" title="Synaptic plasticity">activity-dependent modification of synapses</a> is the brain's primary mechanism for learning and memory.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ShepherdSOB_11-2"><a href="#cite_note-ShepherdSOB-11">[11]</a></sup>
</p><p>Most of the space in the brain is taken up by axons, which are often bundled together in what are called <i>nerve fiber tracts</i>. A myelinated axon is wrapped in a fatty insulating sheath of <a href="/wiki/Myelin" title="Myelin">myelin</a>, which serves to greatly increase the speed of signal propagation. (There are also unmyelinated axons). Myelin is white, making parts of the brain filled exclusively with nerve fibers appear as light-colored <a href="/wiki/White_matter" title="White matter">white matter</a>, in contrast to the darker-colored <a href="/wiki/Grey_matter" title="Grey matter">grey matter</a> that marks areas with high densities of neuron cell bodies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kandel_2000_8-4"><a href="#cite_note-Kandel_2000-8">[8]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Evolution">Evolution</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Evolution_of_the_brain" title="Evolution of the brain">Evolution of the brain</a></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Generic_bilaterian_nervous_system">Generic bilaterian nervous system</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bilaterian-plan.svg"><img alt="A rod-shaped body contains a digestive system running from the mouth at one end to the anus at the other. Alongside the digestive system is a nerve cord with a brain at the end, near to the mouth." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="218" data-file-width="744" decoding="async" height="88" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Bilaterian-plan.svg/300px-Bilaterian-plan.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Bilaterian-plan.svg/450px-Bilaterian-plan.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Bilaterian-plan.svg/600px-Bilaterian-plan.svg.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bilaterian-plan.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Nervous system of a generic bilaterian animal, in the form of a nerve cord with segmental enlargements, and a "brain" at the front.</div></div></div>
<p>Except for a few primitive organisms such as <a href="/wiki/Sponge" title="Sponge">sponges</a> (which have no nervous system)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jacobs_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jacobs-14">[14]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Cnidaria" title="Cnidaria">cnidarians</a> (which have a nervous system consisting of a diffuse nerve net<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jacobs_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-Jacobs-14">[14]</a></sup>), all living multicellular animals are <a href="/wiki/Bilateria" title="Bilateria">bilaterians</a>, meaning animals with a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bilateral_symmetry" title="Bilateral symmetry">bilaterally symmetric</a> body shape (that is, left and right sides that are approximate mirror images of each other).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Urbilateria_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-Urbilateria-15">[15]</a></sup> All bilaterians are thought to have descended from a common ancestor that appeared late in the <a href="/wiki/Cryogenian" title="Cryogenian">Cryogenian</a> period, 700–650 million years ago, and it has been hypothesized that this common ancestor had the shape of a simple tubeworm with a segmented body.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Urbilateria_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-Urbilateria-15">[15]</a></sup> At a schematic level, that basic worm-shape continues to be reflected in the body and nervous system architecture of all modern bilaterians, including vertebrates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> The fundamental bilateral body form is a tube with a hollow gut cavity running from the mouth to the anus, and a nerve cord with an enlargement (a <a href="/wiki/Ganglion" title="Ganglion">ganglion</a>) for each body segment, with an especially large ganglion at the front, called the brain. The brain is small and simple in some species, such as <a href="/wiki/Nematode" title="Nematode">nematode</a> worms; in other species, including vertebrates, it is the most complex organ in the body.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shepherd_4-2"><a href="#cite_note-Shepherd-4">[4]</a></sup> Some types of worms, such as <a href="/wiki/Leech" title="Leech">leeches</a>, also have an enlarged ganglion at the back end of the nerve cord, known as a "tail brain".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup>
</p><p>There are a few types of existing bilaterians that lack a recognizable brain, including <a href="/wiki/Echinoderm" title="Echinoderm">echinoderms</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tunicate" title="Tunicate">tunicates</a>. It has not been definitively established whether the existence of these brainless species indicates that the earliest <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bilaterians" title="Bilaterians">bilaterians</a> lacked a brain, or whether their ancestors evolved in a way that led to the disappearance of a previously existing brain structure.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Invertebrates">Invertebrates</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Drosophila_melanogaster_-_side_(aka).jpg"><img alt="A fly resting on a reflective surface. A large, red eye faces the camera. The body appears transparent, apart from black pigment at the end of its abdomen." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="424" data-file-width="546" decoding="async" height="171" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Drosophila_melanogaster_-_side_%28aka%29.jpg/220px-Drosophila_melanogaster_-_side_%28aka%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Drosophila_melanogaster_-_side_%28aka%29.jpg/330px-Drosophila_melanogaster_-_side_%28aka%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Drosophila_melanogaster_-_side_%28aka%29.jpg/440px-Drosophila_melanogaster_-_side_%28aka%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Drosophila_melanogaster_-_side_(aka).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Fruit flies (<i><a href="/wiki/Drosophila" title="Drosophila">Drosophila</a></i>) have been extensively studied to gain insight into the role of genes in brain development.</div></div></div>
<p>This category includes <a href="/wiki/Tardigrade" title="Tardigrade">tardigrades</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arthropod" title="Arthropod">arthropods</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mollusca" title="Mollusca">molluscs</a>, and numerous types of worms. The diversity of invertebrate body plans is matched by an equal diversity in brain structures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup>
</p><p>Two groups of invertebrates have notably complex brains: arthropods (insects, <a href="/wiki/Crustacean" title="Crustacean">crustaceans</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arachnid" title="Arachnid">arachnids</a>, and others), and <a href="/wiki/Cephalopod" title="Cephalopod">cephalopods</a> (octopuses, <a href="/wiki/Squid" title="Squid">squids</a>, and similar molluscs).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Butler_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-Butler-19">[19]</a></sup> The brains of arthropods and cephalopods arise from twin parallel nerve cords that extend through the body of the animal. Arthropods have a central brain, the <a href="/wiki/Supraesophageal_ganglion" title="Supraesophageal ganglion">supraesophageal ganglion</a>, with three divisions and large <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Optic_lobe_(arthropod)" title="Optic lobe (arthropod)">optical lobes</a> behind each eye for visual processing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Butler_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-Butler-19">[19]</a></sup> Cephalopods such as the octopus and squid have the largest brains of any invertebrates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bulloch1995_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bulloch1995-20">[20]</a></sup>
</p><p>There are several invertebrate species whose brains have been studied intensively because they have properties that make them convenient for experimental work:
</p>
<ul><li>Fruit flies (<i>Drosophila</i>), because of the large array of techniques available for studying their <a href="/wiki/Genetics" title="Genetics">genetics</a>, have been a natural subject for studying the role of genes in brain development.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> In spite of the large evolutionary distance between insects and mammals, many aspects of <i>Drosophila</i> <a href="/wiki/Neurogenetics" title="Neurogenetics">neurogenetics</a> have been shown to be relevant to humans. The first biological <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Clock_gene" title="Clock gene">clock genes</a>, for example, were identified by examining <i>Drosophila</i> mutants that showed disrupted daily activity cycles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> A search in the genomes of vertebrates revealed a set of analogous genes, which were found to play similar roles in the mouse biological clock—and therefore almost certainly in the human biological clock as well.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> Studies done on Drosophila, also show that most <a href="/wiki/Neuropil" title="Neuropil">neuropil</a> regions of the brain are continuously reorganized throughout life in response to specific living conditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup></li>
<li>The nematode worm <i><a href="/wiki/Caenorhabditis_elegans" title="Caenorhabditis elegans">Caenorhabditis elegans</a></i>, like <i>Drosophila</i>, has been studied largely because of its importance in genetics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> In the early 1970s, <a href="/wiki/Sydney_Brenner" title="Sydney Brenner">Sydney Brenner</a> chose it as a <a href="/wiki/Model_organism" title="Model organism">model organism</a> for studying the way that genes control development. One of the advantages of working with this worm is that the body plan is very stereotyped: the nervous system of the <a href="/wiki/Hermaphrodite" title="Hermaphrodite">hermaphrodite</a> contains exactly 302 neurons, always in the same places, making identical synaptic connections in every worm.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> Brenner's team sliced worms into thousands of ultrathin sections and photographed each one under an electron microscope, then visually matched fibers from section to section, to map out every neuron and synapse in the entire body.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> The complete neuronal <i>wiring diagram</i> of <i>C.elegans</i> – its <a href="/wiki/Connectome" title="Connectome">connectome</a> was achieved.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> Nothing approaching this level of detail is available for any other organism, and the information gained has enabled a multitude of studies that would otherwise have not been possible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup></li>
<li>The sea slug <i><a href="/wiki/Aplysia" title="Aplysia">Aplysia</a> californica</i> was chosen by Nobel Prize-winning neurophysiologist <a href="/wiki/Eric_Kandel" title="Eric Kandel">Eric Kandel</a> as a model for studying the cellular basis of <a href="/wiki/Learning" title="Learning">learning</a> and <a href="/wiki/Memory" title="Memory">memory</a>, because of the simplicity and accessibility of its nervous system, and it has been examined in hundreds of experiments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup></li></ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Vertebrates">Vertebrates</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Shark_brain.png"><img alt="A T-shaped object is made up of the cord at the bottom which feeds into a lower central mass. This is topped by a larger central mass with an arm extending from either side." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="362" data-file-width="348" decoding="async" height="177" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Shark_brain.png/170px-Shark_brain.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Shark_brain.png/255px-Shark_brain.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Shark_brain.png/340px-Shark_brain.png 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Shark_brain.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The brain of a <a href="/wiki/Shark" title="Shark">shark</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>The first <a href="/wiki/Vertebrate" title="Vertebrate">vertebrates</a> appeared over 500 million years ago (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mya_(unit)" title="Mya (unit)">Mya</a>), during the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cambrian_period" title="Cambrian period">Cambrian period</a>, and may have resembled the modern <a href="/wiki/Hagfish" title="Hagfish">hagfish</a> in form.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> Jawed fish appeared by 445 Mya, amphibians by 350 Mya, reptiles by 310 Mya and mammals by 200 Mya (approximately). Each species has an equally long <a href="/wiki/Evolution" title="Evolution">evolutionary history</a>, but the brains of modern hagfishes, <a href="/wiki/Lamprey" title="Lamprey">lampreys</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sharks" title="Sharks">sharks</a>, amphibians, reptiles, and mammals show a gradient of size and complexity that roughly follows the evolutionary sequence. All of these brains contain the same set of basic anatomical components, but many are rudimentary in the hagfish, whereas in mammals the foremost part (the <a href="/wiki/Cerebrum" title="Cerebrum">telencephalon</a>) is greatly elaborated and expanded.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup>
</p><p>Brains are most simply compared in terms of their size. The relationship between <a href="/wiki/Brain_size" title="Brain size">brain size</a>, body size and other variables has been studied across a wide range of vertebrate species. As a rule, brain size increases with body size, but not in a simple linear proportion. In general, smaller animals tend to have larger brains, measured as a fraction of body size. For mammals, the relationship between brain volume and body mass essentially follows a <a href="/wiki/Power_law" title="Power law">power law</a> with an <a href="/wiki/Exponentiation" title="Exponentiation">exponent</a> of about 0.75.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> This formula describes the central tendency, but every family of mammals departs from it to some degree, in a way that reflects in part the complexity of their behavior. For example, primates have brains 5 to 10 times larger than the formula predicts. Predators tend to have larger brains than their prey, relative to body size.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:EmbryonicBrain.svg"><img alt="The nervous system is shown as a rod with protrusions along its length. The spinal cord at the bottom connects to the hindbrain which widens out before narrowing again. This is connected to the midbrain, which again bulges, and which finally connects to the forebrain which has two large protrusions." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="720" data-file-width="960" decoding="async" height="188" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/EmbryonicBrain.svg/250px-EmbryonicBrain.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/EmbryonicBrain.svg/375px-EmbryonicBrain.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/EmbryonicBrain.svg/500px-EmbryonicBrain.svg.png 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:EmbryonicBrain.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The main subdivisions of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Embryogenesis" title="Embryogenesis">embryonic</a> vertebrate brain (left), which later differentiate into structures of the adult brain (right).</div></div></div>
<p>All vertebrate brains share a common underlying form, which appears most clearly during early stages of embryonic development. In its earliest form, the brain appears as three swellings at the front end of the <a href="/wiki/Neural_tube" title="Neural tube">neural tube</a>; these swellings eventually become the forebrain, midbrain, and hindbrain (the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prosencephalon" title="Prosencephalon">prosencephalon</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mesencephalon" title="Mesencephalon">mesencephalon</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rhombencephalon" title="Rhombencephalon">rhombencephalon</a>, respectively). At the earliest stages of brain development, the three areas are roughly equal in size. In many classes of vertebrates, such as fish and amphibians, the three parts remain similar in size in the adult, but in mammals the forebrain becomes much larger than the other parts, and the midbrain becomes very small.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kandel_2000_8-5"><a href="#cite_note-Kandel_2000-8">[8]</a></sup>
</p><p>The brains of vertebrates are made of very soft tissue.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kandel_2000_8-6"><a href="#cite_note-Kandel_2000-8">[8]</a></sup> Living brain tissue is pinkish on the outside and mostly white on the inside, with subtle variations in color. Vertebrate brains are surrounded by a system of <a href="/wiki/Connective_tissue" title="Connective tissue">connective tissue</a> <a href="/wiki/Biological_membrane" title="Biological membrane">membranes</a> called <a href="/wiki/Meninges" title="Meninges">meninges</a> that separate the <a href="/wiki/Skull" title="Skull">skull</a> from the brain. <a href="/wiki/Blood_vessel" title="Blood vessel">Blood vessels</a> enter the central nervous system through holes in the meningeal layers. The cells in the blood vessel walls are joined tightly to one another, forming the <a href="/wiki/Blood%E2%80%93brain_barrier" title="Blood–brain barrier">blood–brain barrier</a>, which blocks the passage of many <a href="/wiki/Toxin" title="Toxin">toxins</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pathogen" title="Pathogen">pathogens</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CarpenterCh1_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-CarpenterCh1-35">[35]</a></sup> (though at the same time blocking <a href="/wiki/Antibody" title="Antibody">antibodies</a> and some drugs, thereby presenting special challenges in treatment of diseases of the brain).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Neuroanatomy" title="Neuroanatomy">Neuroanatomists</a> usually divide the vertebrate brain into six main regions: the <a href="/wiki/Cerebrum" title="Cerebrum">telencephalon</a> (cerebral hemispheres), <a href="/wiki/Diencephalon" title="Diencephalon">diencephalon</a> (thalamus and hypothalamus), <a href="/wiki/Midbrain" title="Midbrain">mesencephalon</a> (midbrain), <a href="/wiki/Cerebellum" title="Cerebellum">cerebellum</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pons" title="Pons">pons</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Medulla_oblongata" title="Medulla oblongata">medulla oblongata</a>. Each of these areas has a complex internal structure. Some parts, such as the cerebral cortex and the cerebellar cortex, consist of layers that are folded or convoluted to fit within the available space. Other parts, such as the thalamus and hypothalamus, consist of clusters of many small nuclei. Thousands of distinguishable areas can be identified within the vertebrate brain based on fine distinctions of neural structure, chemistry, and connectivity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kandel_2000_8-7"><a href="#cite_note-Kandel_2000-8">[8]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Vertebrate-brain-regions_small.png"><img alt="Corresponding regions of human and shark brain are shown. The shark brain is splayed out, while the human brain is more compact. The shark brain starts with the medulla, which is surrounded by various structures, and ends with the telencephalon. The cross-section of the human brain shows the medulla at the bottom surrounded by the same structures, with the telencephalon thickly coating the top of the brain." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="432" data-file-width="300" decoding="async" height="317" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Vertebrate-brain-regions_small.png/220px-Vertebrate-brain-regions_small.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/be/Vertebrate-brain-regions_small.png 1.5x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Vertebrate-brain-regions_small.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The main anatomical regions of the vertebrate brain, shown for shark and human. The same parts are present, but they differ greatly in size and shape.</div></div></div>
<p>Although the same basic components are present in all vertebrate brains, some branches of vertebrate evolution have led to substantial distortions of brain geometry, especially in the forebrain area. The brain of a shark shows the basic components in a straightforward way, but in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Teleostei" title="Teleostei">teleost</a> fishes (the great majority of existing fish species), the forebrain has become "everted", like a sock turned inside out. In birds, there are also major changes in forebrain structure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> These distortions can make it difficult to match brain components from one species with those of another species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup>
</p><p>Here is a list of some of the most important vertebrate brain components, along with a brief description of their functions as currently understood:
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_regions_in_the_human_brain" title="List of regions in the human brain">List of regions in the human brain</a></div>
<ul><li>The <a href="/wiki/Medulla_oblongata" title="Medulla oblongata">medulla</a>, along with the spinal cord, contains many small nuclei involved in a wide variety of sensory and involuntary motor functions such as vomiting, heart rate and digestive processes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kandel_2000_8-8"><a href="#cite_note-Kandel_2000-8">[8]</a></sup></li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Pons" title="Pons">pons</a> lies in the brainstem directly above the medulla. Among other things, it contains nuclei that control often voluntary but simple acts such as sleep, respiration, swallowing, bladder function, equilibrium, eye movement, facial expressions, and posture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup></li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Hypothalamus" title="Hypothalamus">hypothalamus</a> is a small region at the base of the forebrain, whose complexity and importance belies its size. It is composed of numerous small nuclei, each with distinct connections and neurochemistry. The hypothalamus is engaged in additional involuntary or partially voluntary acts such as sleep and wake cycles, eating and drinking, and the release of some hormones.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup></li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Thalamus" title="Thalamus">thalamus</a> is a collection of nuclei with diverse functions: some are involved in relaying information to and from the cerebral hemispheres, while others are involved in motivation. The subthalamic area (<a href="/wiki/Zona_incerta" title="Zona incerta">zona incerta</a>) seems to contain action-generating systems for several types of "consummatory" behaviors such as eating, drinking, defecation, and copulation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup></li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Cerebellum" title="Cerebellum">cerebellum</a> modulates the outputs of other brain systems, whether motor-related or thought related, to make them certain and precise. Removal of the cerebellum does not prevent an animal from doing anything in particular, but it makes actions hesitant and clumsy. This precision is not built-in but learned by trial and error. The muscle coordination learned while riding a bicycle is an example of a type of <a href="/wiki/Neuroplasticity" title="Neuroplasticity">neural plasticity</a> that may take place largely within the cerebellum.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kandel_2000_8-9"><a href="#cite_note-Kandel_2000-8">[8]</a></sup> 10% of the brain's total volume consists of the cerebellum and 50% of all neurons are held within its structure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup></li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Superior_colliculus" title="Superior colliculus">optic tectum</a> allows actions to be directed toward points in space, most commonly in response to visual input. In mammals, it is usually referred to as the <a href="/wiki/Superior_colliculus" title="Superior colliculus">superior colliculus</a>, and its best-studied function is to direct eye movements. It also directs reaching movements and other object-directed actions. It receives strong visual inputs, but also inputs from other senses that are useful in directing actions, such as auditory input in owls and input from the thermosensitive <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pit_organ" title="Pit organ">pit organs</a> in snakes. In some primitive fishes, such as <a href="/wiki/Lamprey" title="Lamprey">lampreys</a>, this region is the largest part of the brain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> The superior colliculus is part of the midbrain.</li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Pallium_(neuroanatomy)" title="Pallium (neuroanatomy)">pallium</a> is a layer of grey matter that lies on the surface of the forebrain and is the most complex and most recent evolutionary development of the brain as an organ.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LullFerris1922_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-LullFerris1922-44">[44]</a></sup> In reptiles and mammals, it is called the <i>cerebral cortex</i>. Multiple functions involve the pallium, including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Olfaction" title="Olfaction">smell</a> and <a href="/wiki/Spatial_memory" title="Spatial memory">spatial memory</a>. In mammals, where it becomes so large as to dominate the brain, it takes over functions from many other brain areas. In many mammals, the cerebral cortex consists of folded bulges called <a href="/wiki/Gyrus" title="Gyrus">gyri</a> that create deep furrows or fissures called <a href="/wiki/Sulcus_(neuroanatomy)" title="Sulcus (neuroanatomy)">sulci</a>. The folds increase the surface area of the cortex and therefore increase the amount of gray matter and the amount of information that can be stored and processed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup></li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Hippocampus" title="Hippocampus">hippocampus</a>, strictly speaking, is found only in mammals. However, the area it derives from, the medial pallium, has counterparts in all vertebrates. There is evidence that this part of the brain is involved in complex events such as spatial memory and navigation in fishes, birds, reptiles, and mammals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup></li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Basal_ganglia" title="Basal ganglia">basal ganglia</a> are a group of interconnected structures in the forebrain. The primary function of the basal ganglia appears to be <a href="/wiki/Action_selection" title="Action selection">action selection</a>: they send inhibitory signals to all parts of the brain that can generate motor behaviors, and in the right circumstances can release the inhibition, so that the action-generating systems are able to execute their actions. Reward and punishment exert their most important neural effects by altering connections within the basal ganglia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Grillner2005_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-Grillner2005-47">[47]</a></sup></li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Olfactory_bulb" title="Olfactory bulb">olfactory bulb</a> is a special structure that processes olfactory sensory signals and sends its output to the olfactory part of the pallium. It is a major brain component in many vertebrates, but is greatly reduced in humans and other primates (whose senses are dominated by information acquired by sight rather than smell).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup></li></ul>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Reptiles">Reptiles</span></h5>
<h6><span class="mw-headline" id="Birds">Birds</span></h6>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Avian_brain" title="Avian brain">Avian brain</a></div>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Mammals">Mammals</span></h5>
<p>The most obvious difference between the brains of mammals and other vertebrates is in terms of size. On average, a mammal has a brain roughly twice as large as that of a bird of the same body size, and ten times as large as that of a reptile of the same body size.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Northcutt2002_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-Northcutt2002-49">[49]</a></sup>
</p><p>Size, however, is not the only difference: there are also substantial differences in shape. The hindbrain and midbrain of mammals are generally similar to those of other vertebrates, but dramatic differences appear in the forebrain, which is greatly enlarged and also altered in structure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Barton_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-Barton-50">[50]</a></sup> The cerebral cortex is the part of the brain that most strongly distinguishes mammals. In non-mammalian vertebrates, the surface of the <a href="/wiki/Cerebrum" title="Cerebrum">cerebrum</a> is lined with a comparatively simple three-layered structure called the <a href="/wiki/Pallium_(neuroanatomy)" title="Pallium (neuroanatomy)">pallium</a>. In mammals, the pallium evolves into a complex six-layered structure called <a href="/wiki/Neocortex" title="Neocortex">neocortex</a> or <i>isocortex</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Aboitiz_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-Aboitiz-51">[51]</a></sup> Several areas at the edge of the neocortex, including the hippocampus and <a href="/wiki/Amygdala" title="Amygdala">amygdala</a>, are also much more extensively developed in mammals than in other vertebrates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Barton_50-1"><a href="#cite_note-Barton-50">[50]</a></sup>
</p><p>The elaboration of the cerebral cortex carries with it changes to other brain areas. The <a href="/wiki/Superior_colliculus" title="Superior colliculus">superior colliculus</a>, which plays a major role in visual control of behavior in most vertebrates, shrinks to a small size in mammals, and many of its functions are taken over by visual areas of the cerebral cortex.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Northcutt2002_49-1"><a href="#cite_note-Northcutt2002-49">[49]</a></sup> The cerebellum of mammals contains a large portion (the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neocerebellum" title="Neocerebellum">neocerebellum</a>) dedicated to supporting the cerebral cortex, which has no counterpart in other vertebrates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup>
</p>
