<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sleep">Sleep</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Sleep" title="Sleep">Sleep</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Arousal" title="Arousal">Arousal</a></div>
<p>Many animals alternate between sleeping and waking in a daily cycle. Arousal and alertness are also modulated on a finer time scale by a network of brain areas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kandel_2000_8-20"><a href="#cite_note-Kandel_2000-8">[8]</a></sup> A key component of the sleep system is the <a href="/wiki/Suprachiasmatic_nucleus" title="Suprachiasmatic nucleus">suprachiasmatic nucleus</a> (SCN), a tiny part of the hypothalamus located directly above the point at which the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Optic_nerves" title="Optic nerves">optic nerves</a> from the two eyes cross. The SCN contains the body's central biological clock. Neurons there show activity levels that rise and fall with a period of about 24 hours, <a href="/wiki/Circadian_rhythm" title="Circadian rhythm">circadian rhythms</a>: these activity fluctuations are driven by rhythmic changes in expression of a set of "clock genes". The SCN continues to keep time even if it is excised from the brain and placed in a dish of warm nutrient solution, but it ordinarily receives input from the optic nerves, through the <a href="/wiki/Retinohypothalamic_tract" title="Retinohypothalamic tract">retinohypothalamic tract</a> (RHT), that allows daily light-dark cycles to calibrate the clock.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup>
</p><p>The SCN projects to a set of areas in the hypothalamus, brainstem, and midbrain that are involved in implementing sleep-wake cycles. An important component of the system is the <a href="/wiki/Reticular_formation" title="Reticular formation">reticular formation</a>, a group of neuron-clusters scattered diffusely through the core of the lower brain. Reticular neurons send signals to the thalamus, which in turn sends activity-level-controlling signals to every part of the cortex. Damage to the reticular formation can produce a permanent state of coma.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kandel_2000_8-21"><a href="#cite_note-Kandel_2000-8">[8]</a></sup>
</p><p>Sleep involves great changes in brain activity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kandel_2000_8-22"><a href="#cite_note-Kandel_2000-8">[8]</a></sup> Until the 1950s it was generally believed that the brain essentially shuts off during sleep,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup> but this is now known to be far from true; activity continues, but patterns become very different. There are two types of sleep: <i><a href="/wiki/Rapid_eye_movement_sleep" title="Rapid eye movement sleep">REM sleep</a></i> (with <a href="/wiki/Dream" title="Dream">dreaming</a>) and <i><a href="/wiki/Non-rapid_eye_movement_sleep" title="Non-rapid eye movement sleep">NREM</a></i> (non-REM, usually without dreaming) sleep, which repeat in slightly varying patterns throughout a sleep episode. Three broad types of distinct brain activity patterns can be measured: REM, light NREM and deep NREM. During deep NREM sleep, also called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Slow_wave_sleep" title="Slow wave sleep">slow wave sleep</a>, activity in the cortex takes the form of large synchronized waves, whereas in the waking state it is noisy and desynchronized. Levels of the neurotransmitters <a href="/wiki/Norepinephrine" title="Norepinephrine">norepinephrine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Serotonin" title="Serotonin">serotonin</a> drop during slow wave sleep, and fall almost to zero during REM sleep; levels of <a href="/wiki/Acetylcholine" title="Acetylcholine">acetylcholine</a> show the reverse pattern.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kandel_2000_8-23"><a href="#cite_note-Kandel_2000-8">[8]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Homeostasis">Homeostasis</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:LocationOfHypothalamus.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="250" data-file-width="350" decoding="async" height="157" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/LocationOfHypothalamus.jpg/220px-LocationOfHypothalamus.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/LocationOfHypothalamus.jpg/330px-LocationOfHypothalamus.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/9f/LocationOfHypothalamus.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:LocationOfHypothalamus.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Cross-section of a human head, showing location of the <a href="/wiki/Hypothalamus" title="Hypothalamus">hypothalamus</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>For any animal, survival requires maintaining a variety of parameters of bodily state within a limited range of variation: these include temperature, water content, salt concentration in the bloodstream, blood glucose levels, blood oxygen level, and others.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dougherty_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dougherty-93">[93]</a></sup> The ability of an animal to regulate the internal environment of its body—the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Milieu_int%C3%A9rieur" title="Milieu intérieur">milieu intérieur</a>, as the pioneering physiologist <a href="/wiki/Claude_Bernard" title="Claude Bernard">Claude Bernard</a> called it—is known as <a href="/wiki/Homeostasis" title="Homeostasis">homeostasis</a> (<a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek" title="Ancient Greek">Greek</a> for "standing still").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> Maintaining homeostasis is a crucial function of the brain. The basic principle that underlies homeostasis is <a href="/wiki/Negative_feedback" title="Negative feedback">negative feedback</a>: any time a parameter diverges from its set-point, sensors generate an error signal that evokes a response that causes the parameter to shift back toward its optimum value.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dougherty_93-1"><a href="#cite_note-Dougherty-93">[93]</a></sup> (This principle is widely used in engineering, for example in the control of temperature using a <a href="/wiki/Thermostat" title="Thermostat">thermostat</a>.)
</p><p>In vertebrates, the part of the brain that plays the greatest role is the <a href="/wiki/Hypothalamus" title="Hypothalamus">hypothalamus</a>, a small region at the base of the forebrain whose size does not reflect its complexity or the importance of its function.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dougherty_93-2"><a href="#cite_note-Dougherty-93">[93]</a></sup> The hypothalamus is a collection of small nuclei, most of which are involved in basic biological functions. Some of these functions relate to arousal or to social interactions such as sexuality, aggression, or maternal behaviors; but many of them relate to homeostasis. Several hypothalamic nuclei receive input from sensors located in the lining of blood vessels, conveying information about temperature, sodium level, glucose level, blood oxygen level, and other parameters. These hypothalamic nuclei send output signals to motor areas that can generate actions to rectify deficiencies. Some of the outputs also go to the <a href="/wiki/Pituitary_gland" title="Pituitary gland">pituitary gland</a>, a tiny gland attached to the brain directly underneath the hypothalamus. The pituitary gland secretes hormones into the bloodstream, where they circulate throughout the body and induce changes in cellular activity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Motivation">Motivation</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Basal_ganglia.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="350" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="153" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Basal_ganglia.svg/350px-Basal_ganglia.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Basal_ganglia.svg/525px-Basal_ganglia.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Basal_ganglia.svg/700px-Basal_ganglia.svg.png 2x" width="350"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Basal_ganglia.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Components of the basal ganglia, shown in two cross-sections of the human brain. Blue: <a href="/wiki/Caudate_nucleus" title="Caudate nucleus">caudate nucleus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Putamen" title="Putamen">putamen</a>. Green: <a href="/wiki/Globus_pallidus" title="Globus pallidus">globus pallidus</a>. Red: <a href="/wiki/Subthalamic_nucleus" title="Subthalamic nucleus">subthalamic nucleus</a>. Black: <a href="/wiki/Substantia_nigra" title="Substantia nigra">substantia nigra</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>The individual animals need to express survival-promoting behaviors, such as seeking food, water, shelter, and a mate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup> The motivational system in the brain monitors the current state of satisfaction of these goals, and activates behaviors to meet any needs that arise. The motivational system works largely by a reward–punishment mechanism. When a particular behavior is followed by favorable consequences, the <a href="/wiki/Reward_system" title="Reward system">reward mechanism</a> in the brain is activated, which induces structural changes inside the brain that cause the same behavior to be repeated later, whenever a similar situation arises. Conversely, when a behavior is followed by unfavorable consequences, the brain's punishment mechanism is activated, inducing structural changes that cause the behavior to be suppressed when similar situations arise in the future.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup>
</p><p>Most organisms studied to date utilize a reward–punishment mechanism: for instance, worms and insects can alter their behavior to seek food sources or to avoid dangers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup> In vertebrates, the reward-punishment system is implemented by a specific set of brain structures, at the heart of which lie the basal ganglia, a set of interconnected areas at the base of the forebrain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Grillner2005_47-1"><a href="#cite_note-Grillner2005-47">[47]</a></sup> The basal ganglia are the central site at which decisions are made: the basal ganglia exert a sustained inhibitory control over most of the motor systems in the brain; when this inhibition is released, a motor system is permitted to execute the action it is programmed to carry out. Rewards and punishments function by altering the relationship between the inputs that the basal ganglia receive and the decision-signals that are emitted. The reward mechanism is better understood than the punishment mechanism, because its role in drug abuse has caused it to be studied very intensively. Research has shown that the neurotransmitter dopamine plays a central role: addictive drugs such as cocaine, amphetamine, and nicotine either cause dopamine levels to rise or cause the effects of dopamine inside the brain to be enhanced.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Learning_and_memory">Learning and memory</span></h3>
<p>Almost all animals are capable of modifying their behavior as a result of experience—even the most primitive types of worms. Because behavior is driven by brain activity, changes in behavior must somehow correspond to changes inside the brain. Already in the late 19th century theorists like <a href="/wiki/Santiago_Ram%C3%B3n_y_Cajal" title="Santiago Ramón y Cajal">Santiago Ramón y Cajal</a> argued that the most plausible explanation is that learning and memory are expressed as changes in the synaptic connections between neurons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> Until 1970, however, experimental evidence to support the <a href="/wiki/Synaptic_plasticity" title="Synaptic plasticity">synaptic plasticity</a> hypothesis was lacking. In 1971 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tim_Bliss" title="Tim Bliss">Tim Bliss</a> and <a href="/wiki/Terje_L%C3%B8mo" title="Terje Lømo">Terje Lømo</a> published a paper on a phenomenon now called <a href="/wiki/Long-term_potentiation" title="Long-term potentiation">long-term potentiation</a>: the paper showed clear evidence of activity-induced synaptic changes that lasted for at least several days.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup> Since then technical advances have made these sorts of experiments much easier to carry out, and thousands of studies have been made that have clarified the mechanism of synaptic change, and uncovered other types of activity-driven synaptic change in a variety of brain areas, including the cerebral cortex, hippocampus, basal ganglia, and cerebellum.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup> Brain-derived neurotrophic factor (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/BDNF" title="BDNF">BDNF</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Physical_activity" title="Physical activity">physical activity</a> appear to play a beneficial role in the process.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AirpollutionPhysicalactivity_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-AirpollutionPhysicalactivity-103">[103]</a></sup>
</p><p>Neuroscientists currently distinguish several types of learning and memory that are implemented by the brain in distinct ways:
</p>
<ul><li><b><a href="/wiki/Working_memory" title="Working memory">Working memory</a></b> is the ability of the brain to maintain a temporary representation of information about the task that an animal is currently engaged in. This sort of dynamic memory is thought to be mediated by the formation of <a href="/wiki/Hebbian_theory" title="Hebbian theory">cell assemblies</a>—groups of activated neurons that maintain their activity by constantly stimulating one another.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup></li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Episodic_memory" title="Episodic memory">Episodic memory</a></b> is the ability to remember the details of specific events. This sort of memory can last for a lifetime. Much evidence implicates the hippocampus in playing a crucial role: people with severe damage to the hippocampus sometimes show <a href="/wiki/Amnesia" title="Amnesia">amnesia</a>, that is, inability to form new long-lasting episodic memories.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup></li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Semantic_memory" title="Semantic memory">Semantic memory</a></b> is the ability to learn facts and relationships. This sort of memory is probably stored largely in the cerebral cortex, mediated by changes in connections between cells that represent specific types of information.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup></li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Operant_conditioning" title="Operant conditioning">Instrumental learning</a></b> is the ability for rewards and punishments to modify behavior. It is implemented by a network of brain areas centered on the basal ganglia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup></li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Motor_learning" title="Motor learning">Motor learning</a></b> is the ability to refine patterns of body movement by practicing, or more generally by repetition. A number of brain areas are involved, including the <a href="/wiki/Premotor_cortex" title="Premotor cortex">premotor cortex</a>, basal ganglia, and especially the cerebellum, which functions as a large memory bank for microadjustments of the parameters of movement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup></li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Research">Research</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Neuroscience" title="Neuroscience">Neuroscience</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">"Brain research" redirects here. For the scientific journal, see <a href="/wiki/Brain_Research" title="Brain Research">Brain Research</a>.</div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Henry_Markram_-_Visualizing_Synaptic_Maps_onto_Neocrortical_Neurons.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1861" data-file-width="2322" decoding="async" height="176" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Henry_Markram_-_Visualizing_Synaptic_Maps_onto_Neocrortical_Neurons.jpg/220px-Henry_Markram_-_Visualizing_Synaptic_Maps_onto_Neocrortical_Neurons.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Henry_Markram_-_Visualizing_Synaptic_Maps_onto_Neocrortical_Neurons.jpg/330px-Henry_Markram_-_Visualizing_Synaptic_Maps_onto_Neocrortical_Neurons.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Henry_Markram_-_Visualizing_Synaptic_Maps_onto_Neocrortical_Neurons.jpg/440px-Henry_Markram_-_Visualizing_Synaptic_Maps_onto_Neocrortical_Neurons.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Henry_Markram_-_Visualizing_Synaptic_Maps_onto_Neocrortical_Neurons.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Human_Brain_Project" title="Human Brain Project">Human Brain Project</a> is a large scientific research project, starting in 2013, which aims to simulate the complete human brain.</div></div></div>
<p>The field of neuroscience encompasses all approaches that seek to understand the brain and the rest of the nervous system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kandel_2000_8-24"><a href="#cite_note-Kandel_2000-8">[8]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Psychology" title="Psychology">Psychology</a> seeks to understand mind and behavior, and <a href="/wiki/Neurology" title="Neurology">neurology</a> is the medical discipline that diagnoses and treats diseases of the nervous system. The brain is also the most important organ studied in <a href="/wiki/Psychiatry" title="Psychiatry">psychiatry</a>, the branch of medicine that works to study, prevent, and treat <a href="/wiki/Mental_disorder" title="Mental disorder">mental disorders</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Starrow_1969_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-Starrow_1969-109">[109]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Cognitive_science" title="Cognitive science">Cognitive science</a> seeks to unify neuroscience and psychology with other fields that concern themselves with the brain, such as <a href="/wiki/Computer_science" title="Computer science">computer science</a> (<a href="/wiki/Artificial_intelligence" title="Artificial intelligence">artificial intelligence</a> and similar fields) and <a href="/wiki/Philosophy" title="Philosophy">philosophy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup>
</p><p>The oldest method of studying the brain is <a href="/wiki/Neuroanatomy" title="Neuroanatomy">anatomical</a>, and until the middle of the 20th century, much of the progress in neuroscience came from the development of better cell stains and better microscopes. Neuroanatomists study the large-scale structure of the brain as well as the microscopic structure of neurons and their components, especially synapses. Among other tools, they employ a plethora of stains that reveal neural structure, chemistry, and connectivity. In recent years, the development of <a href="/wiki/Immunostaining" title="Immunostaining">immunostaining</a> techniques has allowed investigation of neurons that express specific sets of genes. Also, <i>functional neuroanatomy</i> uses <a href="/wiki/Medical_imaging" title="Medical imaging">medical imaging</a> techniques to correlate variations in human brain structure with differences in cognition or behavior.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup>
</p><p>Neurophysiologists study the chemical, pharmacological, and electrical properties of the brain: their primary tools are drugs and recording devices. Thousands of experimentally developed drugs affect the nervous system, some in highly specific ways. Recordings of brain activity can be made using electrodes, either glued to the scalp as in <a href="/wiki/Electroencephalography" title="Electroencephalography">EEG</a> studies, or implanted inside the brains of animals for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Extracellular" title="Extracellular">extracellular</a> recordings, which can detect action potentials generated by individual neurons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup> Because the brain does not contain pain receptors, it is possible using these techniques to record brain activity from animals that are awake and behaving without causing distress. The same techniques have occasionally been used to study brain activity in human patients with intractable <a href="/wiki/Epilepsy" title="Epilepsy">epilepsy</a>, in cases where there was a medical necessity to implant electrodes to localize the brain area responsible for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Epileptic_seizure" title="Epileptic seizure">epileptic seizures</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Functional_imaging" title="Functional imaging">Functional imaging</a> techniques such as <a href="/wiki/Functional_magnetic_resonance_imaging" title="Functional magnetic resonance imaging">fMRI</a> are also used to study brain activity; these techniques have mainly been used with human subjects, because they require a conscious subject to remain motionless for long periods of time, but they have the great advantage of being noninvasive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Brain-computer_interface_(schematic).jpg"><img alt="Drawing showing a monkey in a restraint chair, a computer monitor, a rototic arm, and three pieces of computer equipment, with arrows between them to show the flow of information." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="400" data-file-width="632" decoding="async" height="190" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Brain-computer_interface_%28schematic%29.jpg/300px-Brain-computer_interface_%28schematic%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Brain-computer_interface_%28schematic%29.jpg/450px-Brain-computer_interface_%28schematic%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Brain-computer_interface_%28schematic%29.jpg/600px-Brain-computer_interface_%28schematic%29.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Brain-computer_interface_(schematic).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Design of an experiment in which brain activity from a monkey was used to control a robotic arm.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Another approach to brain function is to examine the consequences of <a href="/wiki/Brain_damage" title="Brain damage">damage</a> to specific brain areas. Even though it is protected by the skull and <a href="/wiki/Meninges" title="Meninges">meninges</a>, surrounded by <a href="/wiki/Cerebrospinal_fluid" title="Cerebrospinal fluid">cerebrospinal fluid</a>, and isolated from the bloodstream by the blood–brain barrier, the delicate nature of the brain makes it vulnerable to numerous diseases and several types of damage. In humans, the effects of strokes and other types of brain damage have been a key source of information about brain function. Because there is no ability to experimentally control the nature of the damage, however, this information is often difficult to interpret. In animal studies, most commonly involving rats, it is possible to use electrodes or locally injected chemicals to produce precise patterns of damage and then examine the consequences for behavior.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Computational_neuroscience" title="Computational neuroscience">Computational neuroscience</a> encompasses two approaches: first, the use of computers to study the brain; second, the study of how brains perform computation. On one hand, it is possible to write a computer program to simulate the operation of a group of neurons by making use of systems of equations that describe their electrochemical activity; such simulations are known as <i>biologically realistic neural networks</i>. On the other hand, it is possible to study algorithms for neural computation by simulating, or mathematically analyzing, the operations of simplified "units" that have some of the properties of neurons but abstract out much of their biological complexity. The computational functions of the brain are studied both by computer scientists and neuroscientists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Abbott_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-Abbott-117">[117]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Computational_neurogenetic_modeling" title="Computational neurogenetic modeling">Computational neurogenetic modeling</a> is concerned with the study and development of dynamic neuronal models for modeling brain functions with respect to genes and dynamic interactions between genes.
</p><p>Recent years have seen increasing applications of genetic and genomic techniques to the study of the brain <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tonegawa_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tonegawa-118">[118]</a></sup> and a focus on the roles of <a href="/wiki/Neurotrophic_factors" title="Neurotrophic factors">neurotrophic factors</a> and physical activity in <a href="/wiki/Neuroplasticity" title="Neuroplasticity">neuroplasticity</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AirpollutionPhysicalactivity_103-1"><a href="#cite_note-AirpollutionPhysicalactivity-103">[103]</a></sup> The most common subjects are mice, because of the availability of technical tools. It is now possible with relative ease to "knock out" or mutate a wide variety of genes, and then examine the effects on brain function. More sophisticated approaches are also being used: for example, using <a href="/wiki/Cre-Lox_recombination" title="Cre-Lox recombination">Cre-Lox recombination</a> it is possible to activate or deactivate genes in specific parts of the brain, at specific times.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tonegawa_118-1"><a href="#cite_note-Tonegawa-118">[118]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Descartes-reflex.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="442" data-file-width="418" decoding="async" height="233" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Descartes-reflex.JPG/220px-Descartes-reflex.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Descartes-reflex.JPG/330px-Descartes-reflex.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/8a/Descartes-reflex.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Descartes-reflex.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Illustration by <a href="/wiki/Ren%C3%A9_Descartes" title="René Descartes">René Descartes</a> of how the brain implements a reflex response.</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/History_of_neuroscience" title="History of neuroscience">History of neuroscience</a></div>
<p>The oldest brain to have been discovered was in <a href="/wiki/Armenia" title="Armenia">Armenia</a> in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Areni-1_cave_complex" title="Areni-1 cave complex">Areni-1 cave complex</a>. The brain, estimated to be over 5,000 years old, was found in the skull of a 12 to 14-year-old girl. Although the brains were shriveled, they were well preserved due to the climate found inside the cave.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup>
</p><p>Early philosophers were divided as to whether the seat of the soul lies in the brain or heart. <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a> favored the heart, and thought that the function of the brain was merely to cool the blood. <a href="/wiki/Democritus" title="Democritus">Democritus</a>, the inventor of the atomic theory of matter, argued for a three-part soul, with intellect in the head, emotion in the heart, and lust near the liver.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Finger14_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-Finger14-120">[120]</a></sup> The unknown author of <i><a href="/wiki/On_the_Sacred_Disease" title="On the Sacred Disease">On the Sacred Disease</a></i>, a medical treatise in the <a href="/wiki/Hippocratic_Corpus" title="Hippocratic Corpus">Hippocratic Corpus</a>, came down unequivocally in favor of the brain, writing:
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r996844942">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Men ought to know that from nothing else but the brain come joys, delights, laughter and sports, and sorrows, griefs, despondency, and lamentations. ... And by the same organ we become mad and delirious, and fears and terrors assail us, some by night, and some by day, and dreams and untimely wanderings, and cares that are not suitable, and ignorance of present circumstances, desuetude, and unskillfulness. All these things we endure from the brain, when it is not healthy...
</p><dl><dd><i>On the Sacred Disease</i>, attributed to <a href="/wiki/Hippocrates" title="Hippocrates">Hippocrates</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hippocrates_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hippocrates-121">[121]</a></sup></dd></dl></blockquote>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:1543,_Andreas_Vesalius%27_Fabrica,_Base_Of_The_Brain.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1346" data-file-width="1202" decoding="async" height="190" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/1543%2C_Andreas_Vesalius%27_Fabrica%2C_Base_Of_The_Brain.jpg/170px-1543%2C_Andreas_Vesalius%27_Fabrica%2C_Base_Of_The_Brain.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/1543%2C_Andreas_Vesalius%27_Fabrica%2C_Base_Of_The_Brain.jpg/255px-1543%2C_Andreas_Vesalius%27_Fabrica%2C_Base_Of_The_Brain.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/1543%2C_Andreas_Vesalius%27_Fabrica%2C_Base_Of_The_Brain.jpg/340px-1543%2C_Andreas_Vesalius%27_Fabrica%2C_Base_Of_The_Brain.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:1543,_Andreas_Vesalius%27_Fabrica,_Base_Of_The_Brain.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Andreas_Vesalius" title="Andreas Vesalius">Andreas Vesalius</a>' <i>Fabrica</i>, published in 1543, showing the base of the human brain, including <a href="/wiki/Optic_chiasm" title="Optic chiasm">optic chiasma</a>, cerebellum, <a href="/wiki/Olfactory_bulb" title="Olfactory bulb">olfactory bulbs</a>, etc.</div></div></div>
<p>The Roman physician <a href="/wiki/Galen" title="Galen">Galen</a> also argued for the importance of the brain, and theorized in some depth about how it might work. Galen traced out the anatomical relationships among brain, nerves, and muscles, demonstrating that all muscles in the body are connected to the brain through a branching network of nerves. He postulated that nerves activate muscles mechanically by carrying a mysterious substance he called <i>pneumata psychikon</i>, usually translated as "animal spirits".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Finger14_120-1"><a href="#cite_note-Finger14-120">[120]</a></sup> Galen's ideas were widely known during the Middle Ages, but not much further progress came until the Renaissance, when detailed anatomical study resumed, combined with the theoretical speculations of <a href="/wiki/Ren%C3%A9_Descartes" title="René Descartes">René Descartes</a> and those who followed him. Descartes, like Galen, thought of the nervous system in hydraulic terms. He believed that the highest cognitive functions are carried out by a non-physical <i>res cogitans</i>, but that the majority of behaviors of humans, and all behaviors of animals, could be explained mechanistically.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Finger14_120-2"><a href="#cite_note-Finger14-120">[120]</a></sup>
</p><p>The first real progress toward a modern understanding of nervous function, though, came from the investigations of <a href="/wiki/Luigi_Galvani" title="Luigi Galvani">Luigi Galvani</a> (1737–1798), who discovered that a shock of static electricity applied to an exposed nerve of a dead frog could cause its leg to contract. Since that time, each major advance in understanding has followed more or less directly from the development of a new technique of investigation. Until the early years of the 20th century, the most important advances were derived from new methods for <a href="/wiki/Staining" title="Staining">staining</a> cells.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup> Particularly critical was the invention of the <a href="/wiki/Golgi%27s_method" title="Golgi's method">Golgi stain</a>, which (when correctly used) stains only a small fraction of neurons, but stains them in their entirety, including cell body, dendrites, and axon. Without such a stain, brain tissue under a microscope appears as an impenetrable tangle of protoplasmic fibers, in which it is impossible to determine any structure. In the hands of <a href="/wiki/Camillo_Golgi" title="Camillo Golgi">Camillo Golgi</a>, and especially of the Spanish neuroanatomist <a href="/wiki/Santiago_Ram%C3%B3n_y_Cajal" title="Santiago Ramón y Cajal">Santiago Ramón y Cajal</a>, the new stain revealed hundreds of distinct types of neurons, each with its own unique dendritic structure and pattern of connectivity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PurkinjeCell.jpg"><img alt="A drawing on yellowing paper with an archiving stamp in the corner. A spidery tree branch structure connects to the top of a mass. A few narrow processes follow away from the bottom of the mass." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="715" data-file-width="611" decoding="async" height="257" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/PurkinjeCell.jpg/220px-PurkinjeCell.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/PurkinjeCell.jpg/330px-PurkinjeCell.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/PurkinjeCell.jpg/440px-PurkinjeCell.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PurkinjeCell.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Drawing by <a href="/wiki/Santiago_Ram%C3%B3n_y_Cajal" title="Santiago Ramón y Cajal">Santiago Ramón y Cajal</a> of two types of Golgi-stained neurons from the cerebellum of a pigeon.</div></div></div>
<p>In the first half of the 20th century, advances in electronics enabled investigation of the electrical properties of nerve cells, culminating in work by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alan_Lloyd_Hodgkin" title="Alan Lloyd Hodgkin">Alan Hodgkin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Huxley" title="Andrew Huxley">Andrew Huxley</a>, and others on the biophysics of the action potential, and the work of <a href="/wiki/Bernard_Katz" title="Bernard Katz">Bernard Katz</a> and others on the electrochemistry of the synapse.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup> These studies complemented the anatomical picture with a conception of the brain as a dynamic entity. Reflecting the new understanding, in 1942 <a href="/wiki/Charles_Scott_Sherrington" title="Charles Scott Sherrington">Charles Sherrington</a> visualized the workings of the brain waking from sleep:
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>The great topmost sheet of the mass, that where hardly a light had twinkled or moved, becomes now a sparkling field of rhythmic flashing points with trains of traveling sparks hurrying hither and thither. The brain is waking and with it the mind is returning. It is as if the Milky Way entered upon some cosmic dance. Swiftly the head mass becomes an enchanted loom where millions of flashing shuttles weave a dissolving pattern, always a meaningful pattern though never an abiding one; a shifting harmony of subpatterns.
</p><dl><dd>—Sherrington, 1942, <i>Man on his Nature</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup></dd></dl></blockquote>
<p>The invention of electronic computers in the 1940s, along with the development of mathematical <a href="/wiki/Information_theory" title="Information theory">information theory</a>, led to a realization that brains can potentially be understood as information processing systems. This concept formed the basis of the field of <a href="/wiki/Cybernetics" title="Cybernetics">cybernetics</a>, and eventually gave rise to the field now known as <a href="/wiki/Computational_neuroscience" title="Computational neuroscience">computational neuroscience</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CKS1993_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-CKS1993-126">[126]</a></sup> The earliest attempts at cybernetics were somewhat crude in that they treated the brain as essentially a digital computer in disguise, as for example in <a href="/wiki/John_von_Neumann" title="John von Neumann">John von Neumann</a>'s 1958 book, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Computer_and_the_Brain" title="The Computer and the Brain">The Computer and the Brain</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup> Over the years, though, accumulating information about the electrical responses of brain cells recorded from behaving animals has steadily moved theoretical concepts in the direction of increasing realism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CKS1993_126-1"><a href="#cite_note-CKS1993-126">[126]</a></sup>
</p><p>One of the most influential early contributions was a 1959 paper titled <i>What the frog's eye tells the frog's brain</i>: the paper examined the visual responses of neurons in the <a href="/wiki/Retina" title="Retina">retina</a> and <a href="/wiki/Superior_colliculus" title="Superior colliculus">optic tectum</a> of frogs, and came to the conclusion that some neurons in the tectum of the frog are wired to combine elementary responses in a way that makes them function as "bug perceivers".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup> A few years later <a href="/wiki/David_H._Hubel" title="David H. Hubel">David Hubel</a> and <a href="/wiki/Torsten_Wiesel" title="Torsten Wiesel">Torsten Wiesel</a> discovered cells in the primary visual cortex of monkeys that become active when sharp edges move across specific points in the field of view—a discovery for which they won a Nobel Prize.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup> Follow-up studies in higher-order visual areas found cells that detect <a href="/wiki/Binocular_disparity" title="Binocular disparity">binocular disparity</a>, color, movement, and aspects of shape, with areas located at increasing distances from the primary visual cortex showing increasingly complex responses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup> Other investigations of brain areas unrelated to vision have revealed cells with a wide variety of response correlates, some related to memory, some to abstract types of cognition such as space.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup>
</p><p>Theorists have worked to understand these response patterns by constructing mathematical <a href="/wiki/Nervous_system_network_models" title="Nervous system network models">models of neurons and neural networks</a>, which can be simulated using computers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CKS1993_126-2"><a href="#cite_note-CKS1993-126">[126]</a></sup> Some useful models are abstract, focusing on the conceptual structure of neural algorithms rather than the details of how they are implemented in the brain; other models attempt to incorporate data about the biophysical properties of real neurons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup> No model on any level is yet considered to be a fully valid description of brain function, though. The essential difficulty is that sophisticated computation by neural networks requires distributed processing in which hundreds or thousands of neurons work cooperatively—current methods of brain activity recording are only capable of isolating action potentials from a few dozen neurons at a time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup>
</p><p>Furthermore, even single neurons appear to be complex and capable of performing computations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup> So, brain models that don't reflect this are too abstract to be representative of brain operation; models that do try to capture this are very computationally expensive and arguably intractable with present computational resources. However, the <a href="/wiki/Human_Brain_Project" title="Human Brain Project">Human Brain Project</a> is trying to build a realistic, detailed computational model of the entire human brain. The wisdom of this approach has been publicly contested, with high-profile scientists on both sides of the argument.
</p><p>In the second half of the 20th century, developments in chemistry, electron microscopy, genetics, computer science, functional brain imaging, and other fields progressively opened new windows into brain structure and function. In the United States, the 1990s were officially designated as the "<a href="/wiki/Decade_of_the_Brain" title="Decade of the Brain">Decade of the Brain</a>" to commemorate advances made in brain research, and to promote funding for such research.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the 21st century, these trends have continued, and several new approaches have come into prominence, including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Multielectrode_array" title="Multielectrode array">multielectrode recording</a>, which allows the activity of many brain cells to be recorded all at the same time;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Genetic_engineering" title="Genetic engineering">genetic engineering</a>, which allows molecular components of the brain to be altered experimentally;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tonegawa_118-2"><a href="#cite_note-Tonegawa-118">[118]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Genomics" title="Genomics">genomics</a>, which allows variations in brain structure to be correlated with variations in <a href="/wiki/DNA" title="DNA">DNA</a> properties and <a href="/wiki/Neuroimaging" title="Neuroimaging">neuroimaging</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Society_and_culture">Society and culture</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="As_food">As food</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Brain_as_food" title="Brain as food">Brain as food</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gulai_Otak.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1509" data-file-width="2268" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Gulai_Otak.jpg/220px-Gulai_Otak.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Gulai_Otak.jpg/330px-Gulai_Otak.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Gulai_Otak.jpg/440px-Gulai_Otak.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Gulai_Otak.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gulai_otak" title="Gulai otak">Gulai otak</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Beef_brain" title="Beef brain">beef brain</a> curry from Indonesia</div></div></div>
<p>Animal brains are used as food in numerous cuisines.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="In_rituals">In rituals</span></h3>
<p>Some <a href="/wiki/Archaeology" title="Archaeology">archaeological</a> evidence suggests that the mourning rituals of <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">European</a> <a href="/wiki/Neanderthal" title="Neanderthal">Neanderthals</a> also involved the consumption of the brain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fore_(people)" title="Fore (people)">Fore</a> people of <a href="/wiki/Papua_New_Guinea" title="Papua New Guinea">Papua New Guinea</a> are known to eat human brains. In funerary rituals, those close to the dead would eat the brain of the deceased to create a sense of <a href="/wiki/Immortality" title="Immortality">immortality</a>. A <a href="/wiki/Prion" title="Prion">prion</a> disease called <a href="/wiki/Kuru_(disease)" title="Kuru (disease)">kuru</a> has been traced to this.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup>
</p>
